مراد و جوب حرمت وغیرہ ہے جو مکلف کی صفات بنتی ہیں اور مکلف حادث ہے تو حکم بھی حادث ہے تو پھر جواب یہ ہوگا کہ خطاب اللہ ما خوطب بہ کے معنی میں ہے یعنی حکم وہ ہے جس کے ساتھ خطاب کیا گیا ہو اور ما خوطب به یعنی وجوب وحرمت و غیره حادث ہیں قدیم نہیں تو یہ تعریف بھی حادث کی حارث کے ساتھ ہوئی نہ کہ قدیم کی تعریف حادث کے ساتھ لہذا اعتراض غلط ہے تعریف صحیح ہے۔ اعتراض ثانی کا جواب:۔ او بھی تو تشکیک و تردید کے لئے آتا ہے لیکن یہ بھی تنویع کے لئے بھی آتا ہے یعنی نوع کو بیان کرنے کے لئے اور اس تعریف میں او تنویع کے لئے ہے گویا بالاقتضاء او الخجیر سے حکم کی تنویع کردی کہ حکم کی دو قسمیں ہیں ایک وہ جس میں اقتضاء میں ہو دوسری وہ جس میں تغیر ہو لہذا آپ کا یہ کہنایہ تعریف حرف تشکیک پر مشتمل ہے درست نہیں ہے اعتراض ثالث کے تین جواب:۔ بعض حضرات نے جواب دیا ہے کہ حکم وضعی اگر تعریف سے خارج ہوتا ہے تو پھر کیا ہے خارج ہونے دو کیونکہ ہم اس کو حکم مانتے ہی نہیں نہیں ہماری اصطلاح میں یہ حکم نہیں ہے جو اس کو حکم کہتے ہیں ان پر اعتراض وارد ہو گا (۲) بعض حضرات نے کہا ہم حکم کی تعریف میں او الوضع کی قید لگاتے ہیں لہرا حکم وضعی بھی تعریف میں داخل ہو جائے گا (۳) لوسلم سے تیسرا جواب ہے کہ آپ کا یہ کہنا ہی غلط ہے کہ حکم وضعی تعریف سے خارج ہے کیونکہ حکم وضعی تعریف میں داخل ہے وہ اس طرح کہ بالاقتضاء او الخجیر سے مراد عام ہے کہ اقتضاء صریحی ہو یا اقتضا میمنی ہو تو اقتضاء صریحی میں حکم تکلیلی داخل ہو جائے گا اور اقتضا ضمنی میں حکم وضعی داخل ہو جائے گا کیونکہ حکم وضعی میں اقتضا میمنی ہوتا ہے مثلا دلوک شمس وجوب صلوہ کا سبب ہے یہ حکم وضعی ہے اس میں ضمناً اقتضاء پائی جارہی ہے کیونکہ اس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دلوک شمس کے وقت نماز واجب ہے اور وجوب اقتضاء ہی ہے اسی طرح وضو کا نماز کے لئے شرط ہونے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ نماز کے لئے وضو واجب ہے تو گویا نماز کے لئے وضو کو طلب کیا گیا اسی طرح نجاست کا نماز کے لئے مانع ہونے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ نجاست کے ساتھ نماز حرام اور حرمت بھی اقتضاء کی قسم ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ حکم وضعی میں بھی ضمناً اقتضاء پائی جارہی ہے لہذا اقتضاء کی قید سے حکم وضعی بھی حکم کی تعریف میں داخل ہوگا۔
The intended meaning is obligation, prohibition, etc., which are attributes of the legally responsible person, and the legally responsible person is contingent, so the ruling is also contingent. Then the response is that khitab Allah (the speech of God) is in the meaning of 'ma khutiba bihi' (that with which the address is made), i.e., the ruling is that with which the address is made, and 'ma khutiba bihi' i.e., obligation, prohibition, etc., are contingent, not eternal. So this definition defines the contingent with the contingent, not the eternal with the contingent; hence the objection is invalid and the definition is correct. Response to the second objection: 'Aw' comes for doubt and hesitation, but it also comes for division (tanwi'), i.e., to explain the type. In this definition, 'aw' is for division, as if 'bi al-iqtida aw al-takhyir' divides the ruling into two types: one that involves demand (iqtida) and one that involves choice (takhyir). Therefore, your statement that this definition contains a letter of doubt is incorrect. The third objection has three responses: (1) Some scholars responded that if the wad'i ruling is excluded from the definition, then let it be excluded, because we do not consider it a ruling at all; in our terminology, it is not a ruling. For those who call it a ruling, the objection applies. (2) Some scholars said we add the qualifier 'al-wad'' in the definition of ruling, so the wad'i ruling will be included in the definition. (3) A third response, by way of submission (wa sallam), is that your statement that the wad'i ruling is excluded from the definition is itself incorrect, because the wad'i ruling is included in the definition. This is because 'bi al-iqtida aw al-takhyir' is general, meaning whether the demand is explicit (sarihi) or implicit (dhimni). Under explicit demand, the taklifi ruling falls, and under implicit demand, the wad'i ruling falls, because in the wad'i ruling, implicit demand is present. For example, the declination of the sun (zawal) is a cause (sabab) for the obligation of prayer; this is a wad'i ruling, and it implicitly contains demand because its meaning is that prayer is obligatory at the time of zawal, and obligation is a demand. Similarly, the meaning of ablution being a condition for prayer is that ablution is obligatory for prayer, so ablution is demanded for prayer. Likewise, the meaning of impurity being a preventing factor (mani') for prayer is that prayer with impurity is prohibited, and prohibition is also a type of demand. Thus it is clear that in wad'i rulings, implicit demand is present. Therefore, through the qualifier of iqtida, the wad'i ruling will also be included in the definition of ruling.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) in which the commentator (al-Musannif) responds to three objections raised against the definition of 'hukm' (ruling) as given by the matn author (Sadr al-Shariah). The definition in question is 'kalam Allah al-muta'alliq bi af'al al-mukallafin bi al-iqtida aw al-takhyir' (the speech of God relating to the actions of the legally responsible person by way of demand or choice). The first objection concerns the possibility of defining contingent rulings; the answer is that the definition uses 'ma khutiba bihi' which is contingent, thus defining the contingent with the contingent is valid. The second objection relates to the letter 'aw' suggesting doubt; the commentator clarifies that 'aw' here is for division (tanwi'), not doubt, distinguishing between demand and choice. The third objection is that wad'i rulings (causes, conditions, preventers) are excluded. Three responses are given: (1) some deny wad'i rulings as rulings altogether; (2) some add the qualifier 'al-wad''; (3) the preferred response is that wad'i rulings are included under implicit demand (iqtida dhimni). Examples: sabab (cause) like zawal implies obligation; shart (condition) like wudu implies obligation; mani' (preventer) like najasah implies prohibition. Thus, the definition's 'bi al-iqtida' is general enough to include both explicit and implicit demand.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā'): طلب الشيء من المكلف، سواء كان طلبًا صريحًا (صريحي) أو ضمنيًا (ضمني)
الحكم الوضعي (al-ḥukm al-waḍ‘ī): تعلق شيء بشيء لكونه سببا أو شرطا أو مانعا
التخيير (al-takhyīr): الخطاب الذي لا يقتضي طلب فعل أو ترك بل الإباحة

According to the third response to the objection, how does the wad'i ruling enter the definition of hukm?
A) It enters through explicit demand (iqtida sarih).
B) It enters through implicit demand (iqtida dhimni).
C) It enters only by adding the qualifier 'al-wad''.
D) It does not enter at all.

Key Takeaway
Wad'i rulings are included in the Hanafi definition of hukm through implicit demand (iqtida dhimni), as cause, condition, and preventer all imply a demand.

Adding the Qualifier 'al-Wad'' to the Definition of Hukm
Critical refinement on definition of hukm and wad'i rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi debate on whether the qualifier 'al-wad'' should be added to the definition of hukm, and evaluate the arguments for and against inclusion of wad'i rulings under iqtida.

Core Idea
The meanings of taklifi and wad'i rulings are fundamentally different, so a separate qualifier is needed for a comprehensive definition.

This lesson examines a critical refinement in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the debate over the definition of hukm and whether wad'i rulings (like sabab, shart, mani') are subsumed under the qualifier 'bi al-iqtida' or require an explicit additional term 'al-wad''. The discussion also addresses an objection about omitted types of wad'i rulings and the conceptual relationship between general and specific categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Debate
Recognize that the core issue is whether wad'i rulings should be included in the definition of hukm under the qualifier 'bi al-iqtida' or require a separate qualifier 'al-wad''. Note the two opposing positions.
2
Understand the Objection about Omitted Types
Analyze the objection that the matn author omitted the third type (mani'), and the response that 'mā ʿadā humā' covers all wad'i types. Also note that commentators limited to three types while Hanafis recognize more (sabab, dalil, alamah).
3
Analyze the Argument for Adding the Qualifier
Examine al-Musannif's reasoning: taklifi and wad'i rulings have different essential meanings (takhāluf al-mafhūm) – wad'i is relation of one thing to another, taklifi is direct demand. Therefore they cannot be combined under one qualifier.
4
Evaluate the Counter-Argument
Consider the objection that difference in concepts does not prevent combination, using the analogy of general and specific (amm and khass). Assess the validity of this analogy in this context.
5
Determine the Practical Consequence
Understand that the thamarah al-khilaf is whether the definition of hukm must include 'aw al-waḍ‘' or not. Al-Musannif's tarjih is that adding the qualifier is preferable.

اعتراض: حکم وضعی تین چیزوں کا نام ہے ایک شئی کا دوسری شئی کے لئے سبب ہونا شرط ہونا، یا پھر مانع ہونا لیکن ماتن نے اول دو کو تو بیان کیا ہے تیسری کو بیان نہیں کیا بلکہ اس کو مہمل چھوڑ دیا۔
جواب: ماتن نے حکم وضعی کی تیسری قسم کو چھوڑا نہیں بلکہ بیان کیا ہے کیونکہ ماتن نے محو ہما لفظ بول کر سہیت اور شرطیت کے علاوہ جتنے بھی احکام وضعیہ ہیں ان سب کی طرف اشارہ کر دیا ہے بلکہ حق تو یہ ہے کہ بعض اقسام کو چھوڑنے والا اعتراض خود شاریخ پر ہوتا ہے کیونکہ شاریخ نے حکم وضعی میں صرف تین چیزوں کو داخل کیا ہے حالانکہ ان تین کے علاوہ حکم وضعی کی اور بھی قسمیں ہیں مثلاشئی کارکن ہونا، دلیل ہونا ، علامت ہونا یہ سارے احکام وضعیہ ہیں لیکن شاریخ نے ان کو بیان نہیں کیا۔ وذهب المصنف الى ان الحق:۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے اور غرض شارع ماتن پر اعتراض کرتا ہے اعتراض سے پہلے مقدمہ سن لیں۔
مقدمہ:۔ ماتن نے وزاد البعض سے یہ بیان فرمایا کہ حکم کی تعریف میں حکم وضعی داخل نہیں اور اس کے جواب میں بعض نے تو یہ کہ حکم وضعی بالاقتضاء کی قید میں داخل ہے اور بعض نے کہا یہ بالاقتضاء کی قید میں داخل نہیں ہے بلکہ اس کو داخل کرنے کے لئے او الوضع کی قید لگا نا پڑے گی اور مصنف نے انہی کو ترجیح دی ہے کہا ہے کہ او الوضع کی قید لگانا اولی ہے کیونکہ حکم وضعی بالا قضاء کی قید میں داخل نہیں ہوسکتا اور وہ اس لئے کہ حکم تکلیفی اور حکم وضعی کے مفہوم میں تفاسیر ہے کیونکہ حکم وضعی کا مطلب ہے تعلق شئی بشی اور حکم تکلیلی کا یہ مفہوم نہیں ہے جب دونوں کے مفہوم متفایہ ہیں تو یہ ایک قید میں جمع نہیں ہو سکتے لبد الحکم وضعی کے لئے الگ قید ذکر کرنا ہی پڑے گی۔
اعتراض: ماتن کا دوسرے مذہب والوں کو ترجیح دینا درست نہیں ہے تین وجوہ سے (۱) ایک وجہ تو یہ ہے کہ بعض حضرات خطاب وضعی کو حکم ہی نہیں مانتے بلکہ وہ کہتے ہیں یہ خطاب تو ہے لیکن اس کا نام حکم نہیں ہے تو جب ان کے نزدیک خطاب وضعی کو حکم کی تعریف میں داخل کرنا درست ہی نہیں ہے تو پھر حکم کی تعریف میں قید وضعی کو ذکر کرنا ان پر کیسے واجب ہوگا بلکہ کیسے صحیح ہوگا ا مامانی ۔ آپ نے کہا ہے کہ حکم وضعی حکم تکلیلی کے ساتھ نہیں پایا جاتا اور اس کی وجہ یہ بیان کی کہ دونوں کے مفہوموں میں تغایر ہے یہ درست نہیں کیونکہ دو چیزوں کے مفہوم کا تغایر ان دونوں کے جمع ہونے سے مانع نہیں ہوتا جیسا کہ عام و خاص کے مفہوموں میں تغایر ہوتا ہے لیکن وہ دونوں جمع ہوتے ہیں کہ خاص عام میں پایا جاتا ہے اور یہاں بھی حکم تکلیلی عام کے درجہ میں ہے اور حکم وضعی خاص کے درجہ میں ہے بند احکم وضعی حکم تکلیلی میں پایا جاسکتا ہے تو آپ کا یہ کہنا غلط ہوا کہ حکم وضعی حکم تکلیفی میں نہیں پایا جاسکتا!!! ماتن نے خطاب وضعی کی تعریف تعلق اشتئی بالشئی سے کی ہے یہ بھی درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس کی تعریف یوں ہے الخطاب بتعلق شئى بشئى لكونه شرطاله او سببا او مانعا .
And the author [of the commentary] objects to the author of the matn. Objection: Wad'i ruling is the name for three things: a thing being a cause for another thing, a condition, or a preventer. But the matn author mentioned the first two, not the third, and left it omitted. Response: The matn author did not omit the third type but mentioned it, because by saying 'mā ʿadā humā' (other than these two) he pointed to all wad'i rulings besides causality and conditionality. Rather, the truth is that the objection about omitting some types applies to the commentators themselves, because the commentators have only included three things in wad'i rulings, whereas besides these three there are other types of wad'i rulings, such as being a cause (sabab), a proof (dalil), a sign (alamah) — all these are wad'i rulings, but the commentators have not mentioned them. And the author is of the opinion that the correct view is... This is the second part of the commentary's statement, and the commentator intends to object to the matn author. Before the objection, listen to the premise. Premise: The matn author, by 'wa zāda al-baʿḍ' (and some added), stated that the wad'i ruling is not included in the definition of ruling. In response, some said the wad'i ruling is included under the qualifier of iqtida, and some said it is not included under iqtida but rather needs the qualifier 'al-wad'' to be included. The author [of the commentary] gave preference to the latter, saying that adding the qualifier 'al-wad'' is preferable because the wad'i ruling cannot be included under the qualifier of iqtida. This is because the meanings of taklifi and wad'i rulings are different: the meaning of wad'i ruling is the relation of one thing to another, and the taklifi ruling does not have this meaning. When the meanings are mutually exclusive, they cannot be combined under one qualifier; thus a separate qualifier for the wad'i ruling must be mentioned. Objection: The matn author's giving preference to the second group (those who add the qualifier) is incorrect for three reasons: (1) Some scholars do not consider wad'i speech as a ruling at all; they say it is speech but not called a ruling. So when for them it is not correct to include wad'i speech in the definition, how can it be obligatory to mention the qualifier 'al-wad'' in the definition? Rather, how can it be correct? (2) You said that the wad'i ruling is not found together with the taklifi ruling, and you gave the reason that their concepts are different. This is incorrect because the difference in concepts of two things does not prevent them from being combined, as in the case of general and specific: their concepts differ, but they are combined such that the specific is found in the general. Here too, the taklifi ruling is in the position of the general, and the wad'i ruling is in the position of the specific. For wad'i rulings can be found in taklifi rulings. So your statement that the wad'i ruling cannot be found in the taklifi ruling is false! (3) The matn author defined wad'i speech as 'relation of one thing to another'; this is also incorrect because its definition is: 'speech concerning the relation of one thing to another due to it being a condition, a cause, or a preventer.'
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage continues the critical refinement (hashiya) by addressing a new objection against the matn author's handling of wad'i rulings. The objection claims that the matn author (Sadr al-Shariah) only mentioned sabab (cause) and shart (condition) but omitted the third type, mani' (preventer). The response argues that the matn author's phrase 'mā ʿadā humā' (other than these two) actually includes all wad'i types, including preventers. Moreover, the commentator turns the criticism back on the commentators themselves, noting that they limited wad'i rulings to three, whereas there are more: e.g., being a cause (sabab), a proof (dalil), a sign (alamah). The text then moves to the second part, where the author of the commentary (al-Musannif) expresses his own view: that it is preferable to add the qualifier 'al-wad'' (the declaratory) to the definition of hukm rather than relying on implicit iqtida. He argues that wad'i and taklifi rulings have different essential meanings (takhāluf al-mafhūm) and therefore cannot be subsumed under a single qualifier. A further objection is raised against al-Musannif's preference, pointing out that (1) some scholars deny that wad'i speech is a ruling at all, so the qualifier is irrelevant for them; (2) difference in concepts does not prevent convergence (like general and specific); and (3) the matn author's definition of wad'i speech is incomplete, as it should specify 'due to being a condition, cause, or preventer.' This passage thus presents a multi-layered debate on the proper definition of hukm and the status of wad'i rulings.

Key Terms
الحكم الوضعي (al-ḥukm al-waḍ‘ī): تعلق شيء بشيء كالسببية والشرطية والمانعية
قيد الوضع (qayd al-waḍ‘): اللفظ 'الوضع' أو 'وضعي' يضاف إلى التعريف لإدخال الحكم الوضعي

Checkpoint
Why does al-Musannif prefer adding the qualifier 'al-wad'' to the definition of hukm?
A) Because wad'i rulings are not really rulings; they are just speech.
B) Because the meaning of wad'i rulings is different from taklifi rulings, so they need a separate qualifier.
C) Because the matn author omitted the third type (mani').
D) Because all scholars agree that wad'i rulings are not covered by iqtida.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the distinction between taklifi and wad'i rulings necessitates a separate qualifier in the definition of hukm, reflecting the breadth of shar‘i rulings.

Objection and Responses on Including Hukm Wadi'i in Definition
Objection to matn's tarjih and three responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument for including 'aw al-wad'' in the definition of hukm and distinguish between tabayun and taghayur in usul reasoning.

Core Idea
Hukm taklifi and wadi'i are in tabayun (opposition), not taghayur (difference), so they cannot combine under one restriction.

This lesson unpacks a critical usul dispute: should the definition of hukm (legal ruling) include a separate clause for declaratory rulings (hukm wadi'i) or can they be subsumed under the category of obligation (iqtida')? The Hanafi school insists on separation, and the passage presents three objections and their responses. Understanding this distinction deepens your grasp of usul definitions and the logical precision required in Hanafi legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Pinpoint the exact point of disagreement: whether hukm wadi'i (declaratory ruling) must be explicitly included in the definition of hukm via a separate phrase 'aw al-wad'', or whether it can be covered under the existing phrase 'iqtida''.
The matn (primary text) preferred the separate clause.
2
State the Hanafi position and its rational dalil
The Hanafi position is that hukm taklifi and hukm wadi'i have meanings that are in complete opposition (tabayun), not mere difference (taghayur). This means they cannot be instances of a single category, so a separate clause is necessary.
Proof is rational (aqli): the defining natures are mutually exclusive.
3
Outline the three objections
The commentator raises three objections: (1) Some Ash'aris do not consider khitab wadi'i as hukm at all. (2) Hukm wadi'i can be included under iqtida' like a specific under a general. (3) The matn's definition of khitab wadi'i as 'relationship of a thing with a thing' is incomplete.
Each objection targets a different aspect: existence, subsumability, and definition.
4
Analyse each response
Response to (1): The matn only addressed those who accept khitab wadi'i as hukm, so this objection is irrelevant. Response to (2): The relationship is tabayun, not taghayur, so the general-specific analogy fails. Response to (3): The matn gave the essential meaning (haasil ma'na) to distinguish, not a full definition.
These responses reinforce the Hanafi insistence on precision.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that in Hanafi usul texts, the definition of hukm always includes 'aw al-wad'' to cover conditions, causes, preventers, and signs, whereas other schools may omit or subsume them differently.
This distinction affects how later jurists categorize rulings.

Answer: The matn did not omit the third type of hukm wadi'i but rather stated it, because the matn, by saying the word 'mahwuhuma' [؟], indicated all other declaratory rulings besides validity and conditionality. Rather, the truth is that the objection about omitting some types applies to the commentators themselves, because the commentators included only three things in hukm wadi'i, whereas besides these three there are other types of hukm wadi'i, such as being a cause (sabab), a proof (dalil), a sign (alamat) – all these are declaratory rulings, but the commentators did not mention them. And the author (al-musannif) went to the truth: This is the second part of the commentary's passage, and the commentator intends to object to the matn. Before the objection, hear the preamble. Preamble: The matn stated with 'wa zad al-ba'd' that hukm wadi'i is not included in the definition of hukm. In response, some said that hukm wadi'i is included under the restriction of iqtida', and some said it is not included under the restriction of iqtida', but rather to include it, the restriction 'aw al-wad' must be added. The author preferred these latter, saying that adding 'aw al-wad' is more appropriate because hukm wadi'i cannot be included under the restriction of iqtida'. This is because the meanings of hukm taklifi and hukm wadi'i are different, for the meaning of hukm wadi'i is the relationship of one thing to another, while hukm taklifi does not have this meaning. Since their meanings are distinct, they cannot be combined under one restriction; necessarily, a separate restriction must be mentioned for hukm wadi'i. Objection: The matn's preference for the second school is incorrect for three reasons: (1) Some people do not consider the declaratory address (khitab wadi'i) as a ruling at all; they say it is an address but its name is not 'hukm'. So when, according to them, including the declaratory address in the definition of hukm is not correct, then how can it be obligatory for them to mention the restriction 'wad'i' in the definition? Rather, how can it be correct? Imamani! You said that hukm wadi'i is not found together with hukm taklifi, and you gave the reason that their meanings are different. This is incorrect, because the diversity of meanings of two things does not prevent their combination, just as the meanings of general and specific are diverse, yet they combine, for the specific is found in the general. Here too, hukm taklifi is like the general and hukm wadi'i like the specific; thus hukm wadi'i can be found within hukm taklifi. So your statement that hukm wadi'i cannot be found within hukm taklifi is wrong!!! The matn defined declaratory address as 'the relationship of a thing with a thing'; this also is not correct, because its definition is: 'the address concerning the relationship of a thing with a thing, it being a condition, or a cause, or a preventer.' Answers: Before answering the first objection, understand the preamble. Preamble: Regarding the declaratory address, the Ash'aris have two schools: (1) A group does not consider the declaratory address as a ruling at all. (2) A group considers the declaratory address as a ruling. Then, among those who call it a ruling, there are two groups: one that includes hukm wadi'i under the restriction of iqtida', and another that adds the restriction 'aw al-wad'' to include it in the definition. Answer to the first objection: The matn gave preference and commentary according to the second school; among them, those who add 'aw al-wad'' are better. The matn did not consider the first school, hence the objection is not valid. Answer to the second objection: Your example of general and specific, saying that declaratory address is included in obligatory address, is incorrect, because declaratory and obligatory addresses would be like general and specific only if their meanings had a relation of difference (taghayur), as general and specific have difference. However, the meanings of declaratory and obligatory addresses do not have difference but rather opposition (tabayun). Two things whose meanings are in opposition cannot be combined with each other. Therefore, your analogy of declaratory and obligatory addresses to general and specific is not correct. Answer to the third objection: You objected because you thought that the matn, with 'relationship of a thing with a thing', expressed the meaning and definition of hukm wadi'i, whereas the matn here expressed the essential meaning (haasil ma'na) of declaratory address, that it necessarily requires a relationship of one thing with another. He said this because the matn's purpose was to differentiate between declaratory and obligatory addresses; so he stated the difference: obligatory address does not necessarily require a relationship of one thing with another, while declaratory address necessarily requires a relationship of one thing with another. And the affirmation of the necessary entails the affirmation of the necessitated. Therefore, declaratory and obligatory addresses are mutually opposite. Thus your objection is not valid, because the matn, with 'relationship of a thing with a thing', expressed the essential meaning of declaratory address, not its concept and definition. The concept remains, according to the matn, the same as you stated.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a detailed objection and response discussion on the definition of hukm (legal ruling) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The commentator objects to the matn's preference for adding 'aw al-wad'' (or declaratory) in the definition of hukm, arguing that hukm wadi'i (declaratory ruling) is either not a ruling at all or can be included under iqtida'. Three objections are raised: (1) Some scholars do not consider khitab wadi'i as hukm, so adding it is irrelevant. (2) Hukm wadi'i can be included under hukm taklifi like specific under general. (3) The matn's definition of khitab wadi'i as 'relationship of a thing with a thing' is incomplete. The responses clarify that the matn only addressed the second group (those who consider it hukm), that the relationship is tabayun (opposition) not taghayur (difference) so they cannot combine like general and specific, and that the matn gave the essential meaning (haasil ma'na) to distinguish, not a full definition. The discussion is framed within the Ash'ari schools, but the Hanafi position is the same as the matn's preference. The passage demonstrates the precision required in defining usul terms and the importance of distinguishing between taklifi and wadi'i rulings.

Key Terms
الحكم الوضعي (al-hukm al-wadi'i): A declaratory ruling that establishes a relationship between a thing and something else, such as being a cause, condition, preventer, or sign.
الحكم التكليفي (al-hukm al-taklifi): An obligatory ruling that demands or gives a choice regarding an action, such as wajib, haram, mandub, makruh, mubah.
التباين (al-tabayun): Complete opposition of meanings, such that two concepts cannot combine in a single instance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What reason does the response give for why hukm wadi'i cannot be included under the restriction of iqtida' along with hukm taklifi?
A) Because some scholars do not consider hukm wadi'i as a ruling.
B) Because their meanings are in opposition (tabayun), not mere difference (taghayur).
C) Because the matn defined hukm wadi'i incorrectly.
D) Because hukm taklifi is general and hukm wadi'i is specific.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definition of hukm must include a separate clause for declaratory rulings ('aw al-wad'') because hukm taklifi and hukm wadi'i are in tabayun (complete opposition) and cannot be subsumed under a single category.

Definition of Hukm as Khitab Allah: The Address of the Lawgiver
Citation of a definition of hukm from some mukhtasarat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of hukm as khitab Allah, explain its significance, and apply it to identify the source of shar'i rulings.

Core Idea
Hukm is the divine address — not the ruling itself, but the speech that creates it.

This lesson presents a concise definition of shar'i hukm found in some mukhtasarat: it is the address of Allah (khitab Allah) that indicates a ruling. This definition focuses on the source of the ruling, emphasizing that all legal rulings originate from divine revelation. Understanding this definition helps anchor the concept of hukm in the legislative authority of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'al-hukm' in the passage and note that it is the subject of the definition.
2
Analyze the definiens
The definition states 'khitab Allah ta'ala' (the address of Allah). Consider what 'address' means in this context: the Quran and Sunnah that convey rulings.
This emphasizes the divine origin of rulings.
3
Interpret the phrase 'isharatan ila al-hukm al-shar'i al-ma'hood'
This phrase shows that the definition points to the well-known shar'i ruling. It is not a new concept but a clarification of what is already understood.
4
Recognize the implication
The ruling itself is the effect of the divine address, not the address per se. This definition distinguishes between the cause (divine speech) and its effect (the ruling).
Contrast with definitions that define hukm as the action of the mukallaf.
5
Contrast with alternative definitions
Compare this definition with those that define hukm as 'the address relating to the actions of the mukallaf' or 'the ruling on actions'. This one is broader and includes declaratory rulings.
This definition is comprehensive but requires additional clarification for precision.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage introduces another definition of hukm from some mukhtasarat (abridged works), stating that hukm is the address of Allah (khitab Allah) indicating the familiar shar'i ruling. This is a standard definition in usul al-fiqh. The text then begins to mention that this is clearly stated in many books, but the passage is cut off. This definition focuses on hukm as divine speech, emphasizing the source (Allah) and the subject matter (shar'i ruling). It contrasts with earlier discussions that distinguished between taklifi and wadi'i. The phrase 'isharatan ila al-hukm al-shar'i al-ma'hood' indicates that the definition points to the well-known concept, not a new invention.

Key Terms
خطاب الله (khitab Allah): The address of Allah, i.e., the Quran and Sunnah that convey the shar'i ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definition of hukm as khitab Allah anchors all legal rulings in divine speech.

The Two Meanings of al-Shar‘ī in Defining Hukm
Analyzing the meaning of 'shar‘ī' in the definition of hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two meanings of 'shar‘ī' in the definition of hukm and identify which meaning avoids including rationally known obligations.

Core Idea
The qualifier 'shar‘ī' in the definition of hukm must be read differently depending on whether it defines absolute ruling or legal ruling.

This passage dives into a subtle but crucial point in usul al-fiqh: the precise meaning of 'shar‘ī' in the definition of hukm. The commentator builds on the well-known definition 'hukm is the speech of Allah' and resolves an ambiguity that could lead to including obligations known by reason, like the obligation of faith. Understanding this distinction sharpens your grasp of what qualifies as a genuinely Sharī‘ah-based ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contexts for the definition
The commentator considers two scenarios: (1) the definition is for absolute ruling (al-ḥukm al-muṭlaq), and (2) the definition is for legal ruling (al-ḥukm al-shar‘ī). The meaning of 'shar‘ī' changes accordingly.
The qualifier 'shar‘ī' is not a mere decorative word; its interpretation determines which obligations are included.
2
Analyze the first meaning: 'dependent on revelation'
If the definition is of absolute ruling, then 'shar‘ī' means 'that which depends on revelation (al-shar‘).' This serves as a restricting qualifier to exclude obligations that reason alone can know, such as the obligation to have faith (wujūb al-īmān).
This meaning is used when the definition is intended to cover all rulings, including those not conveyed by revelation.
3
Analyze the second meaning: 'conveyed by the Lawgiver's speech'
If the definition is of legal ruling itself, then 'shar‘ī' means 'that which is conveyed by the speech of the Lawgiver.' The definition would be redundant if we used the first meaning, because then it would include things like the obligation of faith which are not part of fiqh (since fiqh deals only with practical acts).
Here, al-shar‘ī is not a restriction but a description of the source: the ruling comes from divine speech.
4
Apply the resolution to a potential objection
The commentator anticipates an objection: if the second meaning is used, doesn't the definition still include wujūb al-īmān (since it is also mentioned in revelation)? He replies that fiqh rulings are further restricted by 'practical' (al-‘amaliyyah), which excludes purely creedal obligations.
This shows how the scope of fiqh is defined: it deals with practical acts of the mukallaf, not beliefs.
5
Recognize the juristic usage as a metaphor
Finally, the passage notes that in the terminology of jurists, 'hukm' refers to the attributes like obligation and prohibition that are established by the speech—a metaphorical use where the verbal noun stands for the object (e.g., 'ruling' meaning 'the thing ruled').
This distinguishes the usulī definition (the speech itself) from the fiqh usage (the resulting attribute).

قَوْلُهُ ) وَبَعْضُهُمْ عَرَّفَ ) ذُكِرَ فِي بَعْضِ الْمُخْتَصَرَاتِ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى إِشَارَةً إِلَى الْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيّ الْمَعْهُودِ وَصُرِحَ فِي كَثِيرٍ مِنْ الْكُتُبِ بِأَنَّ الْحُكْمَ الشَّرْعِيَّ خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فَتَوَهَّمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ أَنَّ هَذَا تَعْرِيفٌ لِلْحُكْمِ عِندَ الْبَعْضِ وَلِلْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيّ عِنْدَ الْبَعْضِ وَلَا خِلَافَ لِأَحَدٍ مِنْ الْأَشَاعِرَةِ فِي أَنَّ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفَ لِلْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيّ قَالَ الْمُصَنِّفُ هَذَا إِذَا كَانَ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْحُكْمِ الْمُطْلَقِ فَمَعْنَى الشَّرْعِيِّ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ لِيَكُونَ قَيْدًا مُفِيدًا مُخْرِجًا لِوُجُوبِ الْإِيمَانِ وَنَحْوِهِ وَإِذَا كَانَ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيِّ فَمَعْنَى الشَّرْعِي مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ خِطَابُ الشَّرْعِ لَا مَا يَتَوَفَّفُ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ الْحَدُّ أَعَمَّ مِنْ الْمَحْدُودِ لِتَنَاوُلِهِ مِثلَ وُجُوبِ الْإِيمَان مَعَ أَنَّ الْمَحْدُودَ لَا يَتَنَاوَلُهُ حِينَئِذٍ لِعَدَمٍ تَوَقُفِهِ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ . قَوْلُهُ ) فَالْحُكُمُ عَلَى هَذَا أَى عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ أَنْ يَكُونَ خِطَابُ اللَّهِ إِلَخْ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْحُكمِ الشَّرْعِيِّ إِسْنَادُ أَمْرٍ إِلَى آخَرَ لَا خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِفِعْلِ الْمُكَلَّفِ وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ ذِكْرُ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ مُكَرَّرًا لِمَا سَبَقَ مِنْ أَنَّ الشَّرْعِي عَلَى هَذَا التَّقْدِيرِ مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ خِطَابُ الشَّرْعِ لَا مَا يَتَوَفَّفُ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ ، فَإِنْ قِيلَ فَيَدْخُلُ فِي الْأَحْكَامِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ مِثْلُ وُجُوبِ الْإِيمَانِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مِنَ الْفِقْهِ قُلْنَا يَخْرُجُ بِقَيْدِ الْعَمَلِيَّةِ . قَوْلُهُ ) وَالْفُقَهَاءُ يُرِيدُ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِى اصْطَلَاحِ الْفُقَهَاءِ حَقِيقَةٌ فِيمَا ثَبَتَ بِالْخِطَابِ مِنْ الْوُجُوبِ وَالْحُرُمَةِ وَنَحْوِهِمَا ، وَهُوَ مَجَانٌ لُغَوِيٌّ حَيْثُ أُطْلِقَ الْمَصْدَرَ أَعْنِي الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْمَفْعُولِ أَعْنِى الْمَحْكُومَ بِهِ .
His saying: 'And some defined [it]' — It is mentioned in some abridgments that ruling is the speech of Allah Most High, indicating the well-known legal ruling. And it is explicitly stated in many books that the legal ruling is the speech of Allah Most High. The author mistakenly thought that this is a definition of [absolute] ruling according to some, and of legal ruling according to others, but none of the Ash'aris disagree that this definition is of legal ruling. The author said: If this is a definition of absolute ruling, then the meaning of 'legal' is 'that which depends on revelation' to be a useful restriction excluding the obligation of faith and the like. And if it is a definition of legal ruling, then the meaning of 'legal' is 'that which is conveyed by the speech of the Lawgiver', not 'that which depends on revelation', otherwise the definition would be broader than the defined, since it would include things like the obligation of faith even though the defined does not include it because it does not depend on revelation. His saying: 'So the ruling on this' — meaning on the assumption that 'Khitab Allah' etc. is a definition of legal ruling — is 'the attribution of one thing to another', not 'the speech of Allah Most High related to the act of the mukallaf', otherwise mentioning 'legality' would be redundant given the earlier point that 'legal' in this assumption means what is conveyed by the Lawgiver's speech, not what depends on revelation. If it is said: Then it would include in legal rulings the obligation of faith even though it is not part of fiqh? We say: It is excluded by the restriction of 'practical'. His saying: 'And the jurists' — meaning that in the terminology of the jurists, 'ruling' is a reality in what is established by speech of obligation, prohibition, and the like, and it is a linguistic metaphor where the infinitive 'ruling' is applied to the object of the ruling.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of hukm (ruling) as 'Khitab Allah' (speech of Allah). The commentator begins by noting that the Ash'ari scholars unanimously agree that this definition refers specifically to legal (shar‘ī) rulings. The author (al-muṣannif) had mistakenly thought there was a disagreement regarding whether it defines absolute rule or legal rule. The commentator then distinguishes two scenarios: if the definition is for absolute rule, then the qualifier 'shar‘ī' means 'dependent on revelation,' which excludes innate obligations like the obligation of faith (which is known by reason). If the definition is for legal rule, then 'shar‘ī' means 'what is conveyed by the Lawgiver's address,' to avoid making the definition broader than the defined. The commentator also clarifies that on the latter interpretation, the definition is not the speech itself but the attribution of one thing to another (i.e., the content of the ruling). He addresses a potential objection: that this would include the obligation of faith (since it is conveyed by revelation?) — but he responds that it is excluded by the restriction 'practical' (which confines fiqh to acts). Finally, he explains the juristic usage: 'hukm' in fiqh refers to the attributes like obligation and prohibition, which is a metaphor where the verbal noun is used for the object (maf‘ūl).

Key Terms
الحكم (al-ḥukm): In this context, it is defined as the speech of Allah Most High pertaining to the acts of the mukallaf (legal ruling) or, in juristic terms, the attribute (obligation, prohibition) that results from that speech.
الشرعي (al-shar‘ī): Depending on context: (1) what depends on revelation (2) what is conveyed by the Lawgiver’s speech.
خطاب (khitāb): Speech or address, specifically the address of Allah (or the Lawgiver) that conveys rulings.
العملية (al-‘amaliyyah): The restriction of 'practical' that limits the scope of fiqh rulings to actions (a‘māl) of the mukallaf, excluding purely creedal obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the potential inclusion of 'wujūb al-īmān' (obligation of faith) in the definition of legal rulings?
A) By restricting the definition to acts that are 'amaliyyah (practical).
B) By arguing that 'wujūb al-īmān' is not a ruling at all.
C) By changing the meaning of 'shar‘ī' to 'dependent on revelation'.
D) By excluding it from the category of khitab.

Key Takeaway
The definition of hukm as Khitab Allah is a definition of legal ruling; the qualifier 'shar‘ī' is interpreted either as 'dependent on revelation' (for absolute ruling) or 'conveyed by revelation' (for legal ruling), and in the latter case, fiqh rulings are further restricted to practical acts.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Defining al-Hukm al-Shar'i: Scope and Misunderstanding
Clarifying the definition of hukm shar'i and refuting a misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the definition of al-hukm al-mutlaq and al-hukm al-shar'i, and apply the restriction of 'amaliyya to exclude non-practical rulings.

Core Idea
The definition of hukm shar'i is khitab Allah, but must be restricted to practical rulings to exclude wujub al-iman.

This passage from a commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih addresses a common confusion: is the definition 'khitab Allah' meant for the absolute ruling or the legal ruling? The commentator explains that while all Ash'aris agree it is for the legal ruling, the author of the matn correctly notes that some books present it as the definition of the absolute ruling. The key is understanding the role of the word 'shar'i' and the need for the restriction of 'amaliyya (practicality) to keep the definition precise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object of definition
Recognize that the passage is about defining 'al-hukm al-shar'i' and the controversy over whether the definition 'khitab Allah' is for the absolute ruling or the legal ruling.
The absolute ruling (hukm mutlaq) would include things like wujub al-iman that do not depend on the law.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations of 'shar'i'
Understand that if the definition is for the absolute ruling, 'shar'i' means 'dependent on the law' (ma yatawaqqaf 'ala al-shar'), excluding wujub al-iman. If for the legal ruling, 'shar'i' means 'brought by the Lawgiver's address' (ma warada bihi khitab al-shar').
This distinction preserves the definition's precision.
3
Analyze the consequence without the restriction
If 'shar'i' were taken as 'dependent on the law' in the legal ruling definition, the definition would be too broad, including wujub al-iman. The commentator adds the restriction of 'amaliyya (practical) to exclude items like wujub al-iman.
The restriction of 'amaliyya is crucial for fiqh, which deals with practical actions.
4
Understand the jurists' metaphorical usage
The passage explains that in jurists' terminology, 'hukm' is used metaphorically (majaz lughawi) by applying the masdar (hukm) to the object (mahkum bih), meaning the actual obligation or prohibition established by the address.
This clarifies that rulings are the effects of the address, not the address itself.
5
Respond to the objection
The commentator defends the matn against the objection that it misunderstood the Ash'ari consensus, showing that the matn's source (al-Minhaj and its commentary) supports the existence of both interpretations.
The response reinforces that the matn's position is correct based on reliable sources.

الْحُكْمَ الشَّرْعِيَّ خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فَتَوَهَّمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ أَنَّ هَذَا تَعْرِيفٌ لِلْحُكْمِ عِندَ الْبَعْضِ وَلِلْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيّ عِنْدَ الْبَعْضِ وَلَا خِلَافَ لِأَحَدٍ مِنْ الْأَشَاعِرَةِ فِي أَنَّ هَذَا التَّعْرِيفَ لِلْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيّ قَالَ الْمُصَنِّفُ هَذَا إِذَا كَانَ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْحُكْمِ الْمُطْلَقِ فَمَعْنَى الشَّرْعِيِّ مَا يَتَوَقَّفُ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ لِيَكُونَ قَيْدًا مُفِيدًا مُخْرِجًا لِوُجُوبِ الْإِيمَانِ وَنَحْوِهِ وَإِذَا كَانَ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْحُكْمِ الشَّرْعِيِّ فَمَعْنَى الشَّرْعِي مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ خِطَابُ الشَّرْعِ لَا مَا يَتَوَفَّفُ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ الْحَدُّ أَعَمَّ مِنْ الْمَحْدُودِ لِتَنَاوُلِهِ مِثلَ وُجُوبِ الْإِيمَان مَعَ أَنَّ الْمَحْدُودَ لَا يَتَنَاوَلُهُ حِينَئِذٍ لِعَدَمٍ تَوَقُفِهِ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ . قَوْلُهُ ) فَالْحُكُمُ عَلَى هَذَا أَى عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ أَنْ يَكُونَ خِطَابُ اللَّهِ إِلَخْ تَعْرِيفًا لِلْحُكمِ الشَّرْعِيِّ إِسْنَادُ أَمْرٍ إِلَى آخَرَ لَا خِطَابُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِفِعْلِ الْمُكَلَّفِ وَإِلَّا لَكَانَ ذِكْرُ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ مُكَرَّرًا لِمَا سَبَقَ مِنْ أَنَّ الشَّرْعِي عَلَى هَذَا التَّقْدِيرِ مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ خِطَابُ الشَّرْعِ لَا مَا يَتَوَفَّفُ عَلَى الشَّرْعِ ، فَإِنْ قِيلَ فَيَدْخُلُ فِي الْأَحْكَامِ الشَّرْعِيَّةِ مِثْلُ وُجُوبِ الْإِيمَانِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ مِنَ الْفِقْهِ قُلْنَا يَخْرُجُ بِقَيْدِ الْعَمَلِيَّةِ . قَوْلُهُ ) وَالْفُقَهَاءُ يُرِيدُ أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ فِى اصْطَلَاحِ الْفُقَهَاءِ حَقِيقَةٌ فِيمَا ثَبَتَ بِالْخِطَابِ مِنْ الْوُجُوبِ وَالْحُرُمَةِ وَنَحْوِهِمَا ، وَهُوَ مَجَانٌ لُغَوِيٌّ حَيْثُ أُطْلِقَ الْمَصْدَرَ أَعْنِي الْحُكْمَ عَلَى الْمَفْعُولِ أَعْنِى الْمَحْكُومَ بِهِ . ترجمہ: ذکر کیا گیا ہے بعض مختصرات میں کہ بیشک حکم خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ الی آخرہ ہے اشارہ کرتے ہوئے حکم شرعی کی طرف جو معہود ہے اور تصریح کی گئی ہے بہت سی کتابوں میں اس بات کی طرف کہ حکم شرعی خطاب اللہ ہے پس و ہم کیا مصنف نے کہ بیشک یہ تعریف حکم مطلق کے لئے ہے بعض کے نزدیک اور حکم شرعی کے لئے ہے بعض کے نزدیک حالانکہ اشاعرہ میں سے کسی کا اختلاف نہیں ہے اس بات میں کہ بیشک یہ تعریف حکم شرعی کے لئے ہے۔ فرمایا مصنف نے جب ہو یہ تعریف ( خطاب اللہ الی آخر ) حکم مطلق کی پس شرعی کا معنیٰ ہوگا ما توقف علی الشرع پس ہوگی یہ قید مفید نکالنے والی وجوب ایمان اور اس کی مثل کو اور جب ہو مذکورہ تعریف حکم شرعی کے لئے پس الشرعی کا معنی ماورد به خطاب الشرع ہوگا نہ کہ مانتو وقف علی الشرع ور نہ ہو جائے گی حد اعم محدود سے بوجہ اس کے شامل ہونے وجوب ایمان کی مثل باوجود اس کے کہ محدود شامل نہیں ہوتا اس کو ( یعنی وجوب ایمان وغیرہ کو) اس وقت بوجہ اس کے موقوف نہ ہونے کے شرعیت پر۔ قولہ فالحکم علی ہذا :: پس حکم اس پر یعنی اس تقدیر پر یہ کہ ہوخطاب اللہ الی آخرہ تعریف حکم شرعی کے لئے تو وہ حکم جو ماخوذ ہے فقہ کی تعریف سے وہ استاد امرالی امر آخر ہوگا نہ کہ خطاب اللہ تعالی متعلق با فعال المکلفین وگرنہ البتہ ہوگا الشرعیہ کا ذکر کرنا مکرر بوجہ گزر جانے اس بات کے کہ بیشک الشرعی اس تقدیر پر ہے ماورد به الخطاب الشارع کے معنی میں ہے نہ کہ ما توقف علی الشرع کے پس اگر کہا جائے پس داخل ہو جائیں گے احکام شرعیہ میں وجوب ایمان کی مثل باوجود اس کے کہ وہ فقہ سے نہیں ہے ہم کہیں گے نکل جائے گا وہ اعملیہ کی قید سے۔ قولہ و الفقہا ء : ارادہ رکھتا ہے کہ حکم فقہاء کی اصطلاح میں حقیقت ہے ما ثبت بالخطاب میں وجوب حرمت اور ان کی مثل سے اور وہ مجاز لغوی ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ اطلاق کیا گیا ہے مصدر کا مراد لیتا ہوں میں حکم کو مفعول پر مراد لیتا ہوں میں محکوم بہ کو شاریخ کا تبصرہ کہ مائن گو و ہم لاحق ہوا ہے تشریح: قولہ بعضہم عرف: یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے غرض شارع ماتن پر اعتراض کرتا ہے۔ اعتراض: اشاعرہ کی بعض مختصر کتابوں میں لفظ حکم کو مطلق رکھ کر اس کی تعریف خطاب اللہ کے ساتھ کی گئی ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ الحکم خطاب اللہ علی الخ لیکن مراد اس سے بھی حکم شرعی ہے کیونکہ اس پر الف لام عہد خارجی کا ہے اور بعض مطول کتابوں میں شرعی کی قید کی وضاحت کی گئی ہے اور کہا گیا ہے الحکم الشرعی خطاب اللہ لیکن ماتن صاحب نے یہ سمجھا کہ مختصر کتابوں میں جو تعریف کی گئی ہے وہ حکم مطلق کی ہے اور معلول کتابوں میں جو تعریف کی گئی ہے وہ حکم شرعی کی ہے جس وجہ سے انہوں نے کہا کہ بعض اشاعرہ کے نزدیک یہ تعریف حکم مطلق کی ہے اور بعض کے نزدیک یہ تعریف حکم شرعی کی ہے حالانکہ تمام اشاعرہ کے ہاں یہ تعریف حکم شرعی کی ہے۔ جواب:۔ تاریخ کے اس اعتراض کا جواب پہلے بھی دیا جا چکا ہے کہ ماتن نے المنہاج اور اس کی شرح سے یہ استدلال کیا ہے کہ بعض کے نزدیک یہ تعریف حکم مطلق کی ہے اور بعض کے نزدیک حکم شرعی کی ہے اور یہ ہے بھی درست لہذا اعتراض بے جا ہے۔
The legal ruling (al-hukm al-shar'i) is the address of Allah Almighty. Then the author supposed that this definition is of the absolute ruling according to some, and of the legal ruling according to others, while there is no disagreement among any of the Ash'aris that this definition is of the legal ruling. The author said: If this is a definition of the absolute ruling, then the meaning of 'shar'i' is 'that which depends on the law' so that it becomes a useful restriction excluding the obligation of faith and the like. And if it is a definition of the legal ruling, then the meaning of 'shar'i' is 'that which the address of the Lawgiver has come with' not 'that which depends on the law'; otherwise the definition would be more general than the defined, because it would include something like the obligation of faith while the defined does not include it at that time due to its not depending on the law. His statement: 'So the ruling according to this' — that is, on the assumption that 'the address of Allah' etc. is a definition of the legal ruling — 'is the attribution of one thing to another' not the address of Allah Almighty related to the action of the legally responsible person. Otherwise the mention of 'shar'iyya' would be repetitive because it has already passed that 'shar'i' on this assumption is 'what the address of the Lawgiver has come with' not 'what depends on the law'. So if it is said: 'Then examples like the obligation of faith would enter into the legal rulings even though it is not from fiqh?' We say: 'It is excluded by the restriction of practicality.' His statement: 'And the jurists intend that the ruling in the terminology of the jurists is a reality in what is established by the address, of obligation, prohibition, and the like, and it is a linguistic metaphor in that the masdar (the word 'hukm') is applied to the object (al-mahkum bih).' Translation: It has been mentioned in some abridged works that indeed the ruling (hukm) is the address of Allah Almighty etc., indicating the legal ruling that is well-known, and it has been clarified in many books that the legal ruling is the address of Allah. So the author supposed that this definition is for the absolute ruling according to some and for the legal ruling according to others, while there is no disagreement among any of the Ash'aris that this definition is for the legal ruling. The author said: When this definition (the address of Allah etc.) is for the absolute ruling, then the meaning of 'shar'i' becomes 'that which depends on the law', so this restriction is beneficial to exclude the obligation of faith and the like. And when the aforementioned definition is for the legal ruling, then the meaning of 'shar'i' becomes 'that which the address of the Lawgiver has come with', not 'that which depends on the law', otherwise the definition would be more general than the defined because it includes something like the obligation of faith even though the defined does not include it at that time due to its not depending on being legal. His statement: 'fa al-hukm 'ala hadha' meaning upon this assumption that 'the address of Allah' etc. is the definition of the legal ruling, then the ruling that is taken from the definition of fiqh is the attribution of one thing to another, not the address of Allah Almighty related to the actions of the legally responsible persons. Otherwise the mention of 'shar'iyya' would be repetitive because it has already passed that 'shar'i' on this assumption is in the meaning of 'what the address of the Lawgiver has come with', not 'what depends on the law'. So if it is said: 'Then things like the obligation of faith would enter into the legal rulings even though it is not from fiqh?' We say: 'It is excluded by the restriction of practicality.' His statement: 'wa al-fuqaha'': He intends that the ruling in the terminology of the jurists is a reality in what is established by the address, of obligation, prohibition, and the like, and it is a linguistic metaphor in the sense that the masdar (the word 'hukm') is applied to the object (al-mahkum bih). The commentator's remark: Some misunderstanding has occurred. Explanation: His statement 'ba'duhum 'arrafa': This is the first part of the explanation's phrase. The commentator objects to the matn. Objection: In some abridged books of the Ash'aris, the word 'hukm' is left absolute and defined as 'khitab Allah', and it is said that 'al-hukm khitab Allah' etc., but the intended meaning is still the legal ruling because the alif lam is for external reference. And in some detailed books, the restriction of 'shar'i' is clarified and it is said 'al-hukm al-shar'i khitab Allah'. But the author of the matn understood that the definition given in the abridged books is for the absolute ruling and the definition in the detailed books is for the legal ruling, which is why he said that according to some Ash'aris this definition is for the absolute ruling and according to others it is for the legal ruling, whereas according to all Ash'aris this definition is for the legal ruling. Response: This objection has already been answered before: that the author of the matn has deduced from al-Minhaj and its commentary that according to some this definition is for the absolute ruling and according to others for the legal ruling, and this is correct, so the objection is baseless.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and clarification of the definition of al-hukm al-shar'i (the legal ruling) from a commentary on the matn of Tawdih/Talwih. The author (matn) is being explained and defended against a potential misunderstanding regarding whether the definition 'khitab Allah' applies to the absolute or legal ruling. The commentator (sharh) explains that the definition is unanimously for the legal ruling among the Ash'aris. However, the matn's statement that some consider it for the absolute and some for the legal is justified by referring to different books. The key point is that if the definition were for the absolute ruling, the word 'shar'i' would mean 'dependent on the law', which would exclude rulings like the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman) that are not dependent on the law. If it were for the legal ruling, 'shar'i' means 'brought by the Lawgiver's address', and then the definition would be coextensive with the defined. The commentator also addresses the concern that wujub al-iman would enter into the legal rulings, but it is excluded by the restriction of 'amaliyya (practical). Finally, the passage discusses the usage of the term 'hukm' among jurists, where it is a metaphorical application of the source to the object. The objection that the matn misunderstood the Ash'ari position is refuted by showing that the matn correctly cited from al-Minhaj and its explanation.

Key Terms
الحكم الشرعي (al-hukm al-shar'i): In this passage, it is defined as 'khitab Allah' (address of Allah) related to the actions of the mukallaf, but with the clarification that it must be restricted to practical rulings (amaliyya) to exclude things like wujub al-iman.
الحكم المطلق (al-hukm al-mutlaq): The absolute ruling, which would include things not dependent on the law, such as the obligation of faith (wujub al-iman).
المجاز اللغوي (al-majaz al-lughawi): Linguistic metaphor: applying a word (masdar) to its object (maf'ul), as in using 'hukm' to mean 'mahkum bih'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the passage say would happen if the definition of al-hukm al-shar'i is taken as a definition of the absolute ruling (hukm mutlaq)?
A) It would include all divine addresses, including wujub al-iman.
B) It would be more general than the defined because it would include wujub al-iman.
C) The word 'shar'i' would need to mean 'that which depends on the law'.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The legal ruling is defined as the address of Allah concerning practical actions of the mukallaf, and it must be restricted to avoid including non-practical rulings.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
The Dual Meaning of 'Shar'iyya' in Defining Hukm
Explaining the two possible meanings of 'shar'iyya' in defining hukm shari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two meanings of 'shar'iyya' and apply the correct meaning based on whether the definition is for absolute hukm or shar'i hukm.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'shar'iyya' shifts to avoid the definition being broader or redundant.

In defining hukm shari, the adjective 'shar'iyya' can be interpreted in two ways: either as what depends on revelation (ma tawaqqafa 'ala al-shar) or as what is conveyed by revelation (ma warada bihi khitab al-shar). The choice depends on which term is being defined. This lesson unpacks the logical necessity behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'shar'iyya'
From the text, note that 'shar'iyya' can mean (1) ma tawaqqafa 'ala al-shar' (dependent on revelation) or (2) ma warada bihi khitab al-shar' (conveyed by revelation).
The same Arabic term takes different senses based on context.
2
Scenario 1: definition for absolute hukm
If 'khitab Allah' defines absolute hukm (the hukm taken in fiqh's definition), then 'shar'iyya' must mean 'ma tawaqqafa' to exclude rulings like iman that are not dependent on Shari'a.
Otherwise the restriction 'shar'iyya' would be superfluous.
3
Scenario 2: definition for shar'i hukm
If 'khitab Allah' defines shar'i hukm, then 'shar'iyya' must mean 'ma warada' to prevent the definition from being broader than the defined term.
The defined term (shar'i hukm) would be specific, but the definition (khitab Allah) would include non-dependent rulings.
4
Confirm the logical consequence
In scenario 2, if 'shar'iyya' were taken as 'ma tawaqqafa', the definition would be more general than the defined term, which is invalid. Therefore only 'ma warada' works.
This shows the precision of Hanafi usul methodology.

قولہ فالحکم علی ہذا :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے شار یخ ماتن کی عبارت کی وضاحت فرمارہے ہیں۔ وضاحت: اگر خطاب الله الخ حکم شرعی کی تعریف ہو تو فقہ کی تعریف میں حکم سے مراد اسناد امرالی امر آخر ہو گا خطاب اللہ الخ نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ ابھی یہ بات گذری ہے کہ اگر خطاب اللہ الخ حکم شرعی کی تعریف ہو تو فقہ کی تعریف میں الشرعیہ کی قید سے مراد ماورد به خطاب الشارع ہو گا اب اگر فقہ کی تعریف میں ہم حکم سے مراد خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ لیں اور الشرعیہ سے مراد بھی ماورد بہ خطاب الشارع ہے تو الشرعیہ کی قید کا مکرر ہونا لازم آئے گا کیونکہ خطاب الله ... الخ اور ماورد به خطاب الشرع کا معنیٰ ایک ہے جس کی وجہ سے شرعیہ کی قید حکم میں ہی ماخوذ ہے تو اس تکرار سے بچنے کے لئے ہم کہیں گے خطاب
(...and so on) For absolute hukm, the meaning of 'shar'i' is 'what depends on the Shari'a'. So this restriction is useful to exclude obligations like belief in God and the like. And when the mentioned definition is for shar'i hukm, then 'shar'i' means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', not 'what depends on the Shari'a'. Otherwise, the definition would be more general than the defined term because it would include obligations like belief in God even though the defined term does not include them, since they are not dependent on the Shari'a. His statement: 'So the ruling according to this': That is, the ruling according to this estimation that 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of shar'i hukm. Then the ruling taken in the definition of fiqh is the attribution of one command to another command, not the address of Allah Most High related to the actions of the legally responsible persons. Otherwise, mentioning 'shar'iyya' would be repetitive because it was already established that 'shar'i' in this estimation means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', not 'what depends on the Shari'a'. So if it is said that acts like belief in God would enter into shar'i rulings even though they are not part of fiqh, we say they are excluded by the restriction of 'shar'iyya' in the sense of dependence. His statement: 'And the jurists': It means that in the terminology of the jurists, hukm is a reality established by address, including obligation, prohibition, and the like, and it is a metaphorical usage from the linguistic perspective because the verbal noun is used to mean the object. I intend by 'hukm' the object of judgment (the ruled-upon). Commentary: His statement: 'Some defined it': This is the first part of the commentary's text; the commentator intends to object to the author (matn). Objection: In some short books of the Ash'aris, the word 'hukm' is used absolutely and defined as 'the address of Allah...' but the intended meaning is still shar'i hukm because the 'al' is for external reference. In some detailed books, the restriction 'shar'i' is specified and it is said 'al-hukm al-shar'i is the address of Allah'. But the author (matn) thought that the definition in the short books is for absolute hukm and in the detailed books is for shar'i hukm, so he said that according to some Ash'aris this definition is for absolute hukm and according to others for shar'i hukm, whereas all Ash'aris consider this definition to be for shar'i hukm. Response: The response to this objection has already been given earlier: that the matn author argued from al-Minhaj and its commentary that according to some this definition is for absolute hukm and according to others for shar'i hukm, and this is correct, so the objection is baseless. The author said: 'Idha kana' (If it is): This is the second part of the commentary's text. From here the commentator explains the author's statement. First, understand that 'shar'iyya' has two meanings: (1) what depends on the Shari'a, (2) what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver. Also understand that 'the address of Allah related etc.' is according to some the definition of absolute hukm and according to others the definition of shar'i hukm. Explanation: If 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of absolute hukm—i.e., the definition of the hukm taken in the definition of fiqh—then the restriction 'shar'iyya' in the definition of fiqh means 'what depends on the Shari'a', not 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver'. Because if it meant the latter, this restriction would have no benefit; rather, 'shar'iyya' and 'al-ahkam' would refer to the same thing since 'hukm' would mean 'the address of Allah' and 'shar'iyya' would mean 'what is conveyed by the address', and they would be identical. Then the inclusion of the restriction 'shar'iyya' would be superfluous. Therefore, it must mean 'what depends on the Shari'a', and this restriction will be exclusionary for those addresses that do not depend on the Shari'a, such as the obligation of belief in God, etc., for they are included in the address of Allah but are not dependent on the Shari'a. And if 'the address of Allah related...' is the definition of shar'i hukm—i.e., 'al-hukm al-shar'i' is the defined term and 'the address of Allah...' is the definition—then the word 'shar'i' (in the defined term) means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', not 'what depends on the Shari'a'. Because if at that time it meant 'what depends on the Shari'a', then the defined term would be more specific and the definition more general. That is: 'what depends on the Shari'a' (the defined term) would only include addresses that are dependent on the Shari'a, while 'the address of Allah' (the definition) would include both those that are dependent and those that are not. Thus the definition would be more general than the defined term, which is not correct. Explanation of the text: His statement: 'So the ruling according to this': This is the first part of the commentary's text; the commentator is explaining the author's statement. Explanation: If 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of shar'i hukm, then in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means 'attribution of one command to another command', not 'the address of Allah...'. Because it has already passed that if 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of shar'i hukm, then in the definition of fiqh the restriction 'shar'iyya' means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver'. Now if in the definition of fiqh we take 'hukm' to mean 'the address of Allah Most High' and 'shar'iyya' also to mean 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', then the restriction 'shar'iyya' would necessarily be repetitive because 'the address of Allah...' and 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver' have the same meaning. Therefore, to avoid this repetition, we say that 'hukm' means 'attribution of one command to another command', not 'the address of Allah'.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion about the definition of 'hukm shari' (legal ruling) and the two possible meanings of the adjective 'shar'iyya' (legal) when qualifying rulings. The commentator (sharh) is expanding on the matn (base text) by addressing an objection and clarifying the logic behind the various definitions. The key issue is whether 'shar'iyya' means 'ma tawaqqafa 'ala al-shar'' (what depends on revelation) or 'ma warada bihi khitab al-shar'' (what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver). The commentator shows that depending on which hukm is being defined (absolute or shar'i), the appropriate meaning of 'shar'iyya' changes to avoid logical inconsistencies such as the definition being broader than the defined term or redundancy. He also defends the matn author's position that some Ash'ari scholars define absolute hukm and others define shar'i hukm, against an objection that all Ash'aris define only shar'i hukm. The response argues that the matn author's interpretation is correct based on evidence from al-Minhaj and its commentary. Finally, the text explains the consequence for the definition of fiqh: if 'khitab Allah' is taken as the definition of shar'i hukm, then in fiqh's definition the word 'hukm' cannot mean the same, else the restriction 'shar'iyya' would be redundant; instead, 'hukm' in fiqh means 'isnad amr ila amr' (attribution of one command to another). This is a classic Hanafi usul analysis showing logical precision in defining core terms.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي (hukm shari): A legal ruling that is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver (ma warada bihi khitab al-shar').
شرعية (shar'iyya): Adjective with two possible meanings: (1) ma tawaqqafa 'ala al-shar' (dependent on revelation), (2) ma warada bihi khitab al-shar' (conveyed by revelation).
خطاب الله (khitab Allah): The address of Allah, considered either as the definition of absolute hukm or shar'i hukm depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if 'khitab Allah' is taken as the definition of shar'i hukm, what must 'shar'iyya' in the definition of fiqh mean?
A) Ma tawaqqafa 'ala al-shar'
B) Ma warada bihi khitab al-shar'
C) Both A and B
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'shar'iyya' in the definition of hukm is not fixed; it shifts between 'dependent on Shari'a' and 'conveyed by Shari'a' to preserve logical consistency in definitions.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Ash'ari Dispute: Absolute vs. Shar'i Hukm
Responding to an objection about the interpretation of Ash'ari definitions of hukm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi matn author's position against the objector's claim regarding the intended meaning of 'khitab Allah' in Ash'ari definitions.

Core Idea
The matn author correctly identifies two Ash'ari views—some defining absolute hukm, others shar'i hukm—based on evidence from al-Minhaj.

An objector argues that all Ash'aris define only shar'i hukm when they say 'al-hukm khitab Allah', contrary to the matn author's claim that some define absolute hukm. The text defends the matn author by citing al-Minhaj and its commentary, showing the distinction is well-established. This lesson examines the structure of the dispute and its relevance to the scope of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the objector's claim
The objector says that in all Ash'ari books, the definition 'al-hukm khitab Allah' is intended for shar'i hukm only, because the 'al' in 'al-hukm' is for external reference (al-'ahd al-khariji).
This would mean the matn author's assertion that some Ash'aris define absolute hukm is mistaken.
2
Identify the matn author's response
The matn author, relying on al-Minhaj and its commentary, holds that there are in fact two distinct definitions: some Ash'aris define absolute hukm and others define shar'i hukm.
The evidence from al-Minhaj is considered authoritative.
3
Analyze why the objection is rejected
The objection is baseless because the matn author's source explicitly distinguishes between the two; thus the objector's generalization is incorrect.
This defense maintains the integrity of the matn.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence affects the definition of fiqh: if absolute hukm is defined as khitab Allah, then fiqh's 'shar'iyya' must be 'ma tawaqqafa' to exclude iman; if shar'i hukm is defined as khitab Allah, then fiqh's 'hukm' must be reinterpreted as isnad amr ila amr.
This shows how usul disputes have downstream effects on fiqh.

متن کی وضاحت
قولہ فالحکم علی ہذا :۔ یہ شرح کی عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے شار یخ ماتن کی عبارت کی وضاحت فرمارہے ہیں۔ وضاحت: اگر خطاب الله الخ حکم شرعی کی تعریف ہو تو فقہ کی تعریف میں حکم سے مراد اسناد امرالی امر آخر ہو گا خطاب اللہ الخ نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ ابھی یہ بات گذری ہے کہ اگر خطاب اللہ الخ حکم شرعی کی تعریف ہو تو فقہ کی تعریف میں الشرعیہ کی قید سے مراد ماورد به خطاب الشارع ہو گا اب اگر فقہ کی تعریف میں ہم حکم سے مراد خطاب اللہ تعالیٰ لیں اور الشرعیہ سے مراد بھی ماورد بہ خطاب الشارع ہے تو الشرعیہ کی قید کا مکرر ہونا لازم آئے گا کیونکہ خطاب الله ... الخ اور ماورد به خطاب الشرع کا معنیٰ ایک ہے جس کی وجہ سے شرعیہ کی قید حکم میں ہی ماخوذ ہے تو اس تکرار سے بچنے کے لئے ہم کہیں گے خطاب
(...and so on) For absolute hukm, the meaning of 'shar'i' is 'what depends on the Shari'a'. So this restriction is useful to exclude obligations like belief in God and the like. And when the mentioned definition is for shar'i hukm, then 'shar'i' means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', not 'what depends on the Shari'a'. Otherwise, the definition would be more general than the defined term because it would include obligations like belief in God even though the defined term does not include them, since they are not dependent on the Shari'a. His statement: 'So the ruling according to this': That is, the ruling according to this estimation that 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of shar'i hukm. Then the ruling taken in the definition of fiqh is the attribution of one command to another command, not the address of Allah Most High related to the actions of the legally responsible persons. Otherwise, mentioning 'shar'iyya' would be repetitive because it was already established that 'shar'i' in this estimation means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', not 'what depends on the Shari'a'. So if it is said that acts like belief in God would enter into shar'i rulings even though they are not part of fiqh, we say they are excluded by the restriction of 'shar'iyya' in the sense of dependence. His statement: 'And the jurists': It means that in the terminology of the jurists, hukm is a reality established by address, including obligation, prohibition, and the like, and it is a metaphorical usage from the linguistic perspective because the verbal noun is used to mean the object. I intend by 'hukm' the object of judgment (the ruled-upon). Commentary: His statement: 'Some defined it': This is the first part of the commentary's text; the commentator intends to object to the author (matn). Objection: In some short books of the Ash'aris, the word 'hukm' is used absolutely and defined as 'the address of Allah...' but the intended meaning is still shar'i hukm because the 'al' is for external reference. In some detailed books, the restriction 'shar'i' is specified and it is said 'al-hukm al-shar'i is the address of Allah'. But the author (matn) thought that the definition in the short books is for absolute hukm and in the detailed books is for shar'i hukm, so he said that according to some Ash'aris this definition is for absolute hukm and according to others for shar'i hukm, whereas all Ash'aris consider this definition to be for shar'i hukm. Response: The response to this objection has already been given earlier: that the matn author argued from al-Minhaj and its commentary that according to some this definition is for absolute hukm and according to others for shar'i hukm, and this is correct, so the objection is baseless. The author said: 'Idha kana' (If it is): This is the second part of the commentary's text. From here the commentator explains the author's statement. First, understand that 'shar'iyya' has two meanings: (1) what depends on the Shari'a, (2) what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver. Also understand that 'the address of Allah related etc.' is according to some the definition of absolute hukm and according to others the definition of shar'i hukm. Explanation: If 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of absolute hukm—i.e., the definition of the hukm taken in the definition of fiqh—then the restriction 'shar'iyya' in the definition of fiqh means 'what depends on the Shari'a', not 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver'. Because if it meant the latter, this restriction would have no benefit; rather, 'shar'iyya' and 'al-ahkam' would refer to the same thing since 'hukm' would mean 'the address of Allah' and 'shar'iyya' would mean 'what is conveyed by the address', and they would be identical. Then the inclusion of the restriction 'shar'iyya' would be superfluous. Therefore, it must mean 'what depends on the Shari'a', and this restriction will be exclusionary for those addresses that do not depend on the Shari'a, such as the obligation of belief in God, etc., for they are included in the address of Allah but are not dependent on the Shari'a. And if 'the address of Allah related...' is the definition of shar'i hukm—i.e., 'al-hukm al-shar'i' is the defined term and 'the address of Allah...' is the definition—then the word 'shar'i' (in the defined term) means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', not 'what depends on the Shari'a'. Because if at that time it meant 'what depends on the Shari'a', then the defined term would be more specific and the definition more general. That is: 'what depends on the Shari'a' (the defined term) would only include addresses that are dependent on the Shari'a, while 'the address of Allah' (the definition) would include both those that are dependent and those that are not. Thus the definition would be more general than the defined term, which is not correct. Explanation of the text: His statement: 'So the ruling according to this': This is the first part of the commentary's text; the commentator is explaining the author's statement. Explanation: If 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of shar'i hukm, then in the definition of fiqh, 'hukm' means 'attribution of one command to another command', not 'the address of Allah...'. Because it has already passed that if 'the address of Allah...' is the definition of shar'i hukm, then in the definition of fiqh the restriction 'shar'iyya' means 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver'. Now if in the definition of fiqh we take 'hukm' to mean 'the address of Allah Most High' and 'shar'iyya' also to mean 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver', then the restriction 'shar'iyya' would necessarily be repetitive because 'the address of Allah...' and 'what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver' have the same meaning. Therefore, to avoid this repetition, we say that 'hukm' means 'attribution of one command to another command', not 'the address of Allah'.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion about the definition of 'hukm shari' (legal ruling) and the two possible meanings of the adjective 'shar'iyya' (legal) when qualifying rulings. The commentator (sharh) is expanding on the matn (base text) by addressing an objection and clarifying the logic behind the various definitions. The key issue is whether 'shar'iyya' means 'ma tawaqqafa 'ala al-shar'' (what depends on revelation) or 'ma warada bihi khitab al-shar'' (what is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver). The commentator shows that depending on which hukm is being defined (absolute or shar'i), the appropriate meaning of 'shar'iyya' changes to avoid logical inconsistencies such as the definition being broader than the defined term or redundancy. He also defends the matn author's position that some Ash'ari scholars define absolute hukm and others define shar'i hukm, against an objection that all Ash'aris define only shar'i hukm. The response argues that the matn author's interpretation is correct based on evidence from al-Minhaj and its commentary. Finally, the text explains the consequence for the definition of fiqh: if 'khitab Allah' is taken as the definition of shar'i hukm, then in fiqh's definition the word 'hukm' cannot mean the same, else the restriction 'shar'iyya' would be redundant; instead, 'hukm' in fiqh means 'isnad amr ila amr' (attribution of one command to another). This is a classic Hanafi usul analysis showing logical precision in defining core terms.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي (hukm shari): A legal ruling that is conveyed by the address of the Lawgiver (ma warada bihi khitab al-shar').
شرعية (shar'iyya): Adjective with two possible meanings: (1) ma tawaqqafa 'ala al-shar' (dependent on revelation), (2) ma warada bihi khitab al-shar' (conveyed by revelation).
خطاب الله (khitab Allah): The address of Allah, considered either as the definition of absolute hukm or shar'i hukm depending on context.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the objector's claim that all Ash'aris define only shar'i hukm rejected?
A) Because the 'al' is not for external reference
B) Because al-Minhaj and its commentary show two distinct definitions
C) Because the matn author says so
D) Because the definition of fiqh requires it

Key Takeaway
Valid ikhtilaf exists among Ash'aris on whether 'khitab Allah' defines absolute or shar'i hukm, and the Hanafi matn author's position is supported by authoritative textual evidence from al-Minhaj.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
